A Distinct and Faithful ACCOUNT OF ALL THE RECEIPTS, Disbursments, and
Remainder OF THE MONEYS COLLECTED IN ENGLAND, WALES and IRELAND, For the
Relief of the poor distressed PROTESTANTS IN THE Valleys of Piemont, Together
with a Brief Account of the present State of Affairs in those Parts; As well
for the better Satisfaction of all those who have already contributed, as for
the Encouragement of those who have as yet been wanting toward so good and
charitable a Work.

Printed and Published by special Order of His Highness and the Council.

LONDON: Printed by HEN. HILLS and JOHN FIELD, Printers to His Highness. Anno
Dom. 1658.

A NARRATIVE Published by the special Order of His Highness The Lord Protector,
AND HIS COUNCIL; For the better Satisfaction of all those who have already
contributed toward the Relief of the Poor Protestants of the Valleys of
Piemont, and for the Encouragement of those who are yet behind to the
performance of so good and charitable a Work.
HIs Highness the Lord Protector having received intelligence about the month
of May, in the year of our Lord 1655. that many hundreds of the Poor
Protestants in the Valleys of Piemont (otherwise known by the name of
Waldenses) within the Territories of the Duke of Savoy, were most cruelly
massacred by a Popish party; and having upon his spirit a deep sense of their
Calamities, which were occasioned by their faithful Adherence to the profession
of the Reformed Religion, was pleased, not only to mediate by most pathetick
Letters in their behalf, to the King of France, and Duke of Savoy, but did also
seriously invite the People of this Nation to seek the Lord by Prayer and
Humiliation in reference to their then sad condition, and future relief: And
from a confidence that the good People of this Nation would be sensibly touched
with the afflictions of Joseph, and in that day of their Brethrens trouble,
manifest a suitable resentment of, and sympathizing with the Sufferings of their
fellow members, professing the same Faith and Religion with themselves, did
forthwith publish a Declaration, expressing his earnest desire that the People
might be stirred up to a free and liberal Contribution toward their Succor and
Support: For the Management of which Collection, certain Instructions were also
agreed upon, and annexed to the said Declaration: And for the more effectual
promoting of the Work, His Highness appointed a Committee, consisting of
persons of known Honor, Fidelity and Integrity, to consider and advise from
time to time how the Moneys that should be thereupon raised, might be employed
with most advantage and certainty for the Supply of those poor distressed
Members of Christ, according to the true intention of the Givers; amongst whom
likewise there were two select Persons of very considerable Estate and
Reputation, appointed to be Treasurers for the receiving in the said Moneys,
whose names, together with the number and names of the Committee abovesaid, for
the better satisfaction of the Reader are here inserted, viz.

Lords of His Highness most Honorable Privy Council.
LOrd Commissioner Fiennes. Earl of Mulgrave. Lord Fleetwood. Lord Viscount
Lisle. Sir Gilbert Pickering. Sir Charles Wolsley. General Disbrow. General
Montagu. Colonel Sydenham. Lord Strickland. Major General Skippon. Colonel
Philip Jones. Mr. Rouse. Mr. Secretary Thurloe.
Other Lords and Gentlemen.
LOrd Richard Cromwell. Lord Lambert. Lord Chief Justice Glynn. Lord Chief
Justice St. John. Lord Whitlock. Will. Steel Lord Chancellor of Ireland. Lord
Widdrington. William Pierpont Esquire Sir Gilbert Gerrard Baronet. Sir John Trevor
Knight. John Crew Esquire Major William Packer. Colonel John Bright. Colonel
William Purefoy. Colonel Tobias Bridge. Colonel John Bridges. Edward Cresset
Esquire
Ministers.
Mr. Philip Nigh. Mr. Edmund Calamy. Mr. Joseph Caryl. Mr. Nicholas Lockier. Mr.
William Ienkins. Mr. Peter Sterry. Mr. Daniel Dike. Mr. Thomas Harrison.
Treasurers.
Sir Thomas Vyner Knight and Alderman of London. Sir Christopher Pack Knight
and Alderman of London.
Neither was this His Highness Christian care and tenderness left unanswered by
the open hearts and hands of many charitable and compassionate souls, both of
this Nation and Ireland, who upon the publishing of the abovesaid Declaration
and Instructions, did most freely and cheerfully Contribute toward the
seasonable Refreshing the Bowels of their poor afflicted Brethren.

True it is, some Parishes and particular Persons of note there were, amongst
whom there was at that time observed some kind of backwardness to this good
Work; (yea many there are to this very day, who do not appear within the
Returns to have contributed any thing toward it) however means were also used
for supplying that defect, and that by a subsequent Proclamation, which was
afterward reinforced by His Highness punctual Orders to the Justices of Peace
in the several Counties, to examine in their respective Divisions at what
places no Collections had been made; and in case of Collections made, where the
Money had not been sent up, and to cause a speedy return to be made thereof; By
which means, through the great goodness of God, and blessing upon His Highness
endeavors (though it may be justly feared that there were defaults in some
places, and the money not to this day delivered in to the Treasurers) there
were considerable Sums in a short space of time brought in, and carefully
lodged in the Treasury, ready to be issued out and distributed, as there should
be ways and means found for the safe conveyance thereof into the respective
places, where those poor wretches had then their abode, which was in the Clefts
of ragged Rocks, and in the Caves of snowy Mountains.

For this very end and purpose His Highness (continuing His care for carrying
on this Work) did at the same time constitute and appoint Mr. Pell (His
Resident with the Protestant Cantons of Switzerland) and Mr. Morland (whom he
had not long before sent as His Envoy to the King of France and Duke of Savoy,
in their behalf) as His Joint- Commissioners Extraordinary for the management
of this Affair beyond Sea, who were likewise furnished with suitable
Instructions and Commands, to the end that by their special inspection and
care, the collected Moneys might not only be remitted from England into the
Valleys, with expedition, security, and at the easiest rates; but likewise,
that being thus conveyed, they might be actually distributed and laid out for
the clothing of the Naked, the feeding of the Hungry, and relieving the Sick,
and that with all possible equity and just proportion, having a due respect to
the greatest Sufferers, and most necessitous amongst them; the which was
accordingly performed by the abovesaid Mr. Pell and Mr. Morland; and the way
and method which they then propounded to His Highness and the Council, being
approved, things were immediately put in execution; and His Highness, upon
advice from time to time with the said Committee, did Order the remitting of
several sums of Money into the Valleys, through the hands first of Mr. Pell and
Mr. Morland jointly, and afterward through the hands of Mr. Morland singly
(who by His Highness Order resided at Geneva for the space of a whole year,
commencing from the twentieth of November 1655. for that very end) according to
the several Conjunctures, and pressing Seasons of the poor peoples Necessities,
as they were by him from time to time represented to His Highness and the
Council.

Upon Mr. Morlands Return into England, an Account was by him tendered to the
said Committee in January last, for the Moneys so remitted beyond Sea, and then
a Sub-Committee was appointed to receive and examine the same ( viz. Sir Thomas
Vyner, Sir Christopher Pack, and Mr. Cresset) who had also power to call to
their assistance any such person as they should think fit as an Auditor, for
the punctual Examination and Adjusting of the said Account; which Sub-Committee
did on the 21 of January last past, make Report to the said Committee, That
they had perused and examined the Account, both as to the Moneys sent from
hence, and that which was by him taken up there, and that upon the whole, they
found it to be justly stated in the Book delivered by Mr. Morland.

Now to the end that public notice may be given what Moneys have been
received upon the said Collections, and from what particular persons, parishes
or places within this Nation and Ireland, what Sums have been issued out and
transmitted beyond Sea, and at what rates; what method has been from time to
time observed in the management of this Affair, and the reason why the same
has been so distributed amongst the poor people by parts, ^^ther then the
whole Sums together; and lastly what yet remains in stock, with the method in
general which His Highness has proposed for the employment thereof for the
best advantage of those distressed Protestants: And to the end that all the
good People of this Commonwealth may receive full satisfaction, and be well
assured that no part of the said collected moneys either has been, or shall
hereafter be diverted from the charitable intention of the Donors, but solely
employed and disposed to those very Uses to which they were originally
designed, His Highness has thought fit to appoint the publishing of the
following Account.

A Distinct, Clear and Exact ACCOUNT As well of what was given by His HIGHNESS
in particular, upon the first News of their Troubles, as of all other Moneys
brought in and received for the Relief of the poor Protestants in the Valleys
of Piemont, Together with the Names of all, as well Parishes and Chapels, as
other Places and Congregations in the several Counties of England, Wales and
Ireland, from whence there were any Returns made (excepting only some few
Parishes and Places in several Counties, whose Moneys were returned without
making any specification of their respective Names.)
To which is added a Catalogue of the Names of several particular persons who
then contributed more largely upon that occasion, with the respective Sums by
each of them so given.
Delivered in to the Right Honorable Commissioners appointed by His Highness
and the Council, for the Management of that Affair, in the Month of November
1657. by Sir Thomas Vyner and Sir Christopher Pack, Knights; Treasurers for the
receiving and issuing forth the said collected Moneys.
l. s. d. 1655. GIven by His HIGHNESS in particular, upon the first News of
their Troubles 2000 00 00

At a general Collection afterward within the Cit^es of London and
Westminster, and the Weekly Bi^ls of Mortality.

AN EXTRACT, OR, Abbreviate of the Account
Delivered in to the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners for the Valleys
of Piemont, by Samuel Morland Esquire as well of the Moneys that were remitted from
England, to the poor Protestants of the said Valleys, before, and during the
time of his residing in Geneva, in Quality of His Highness Commissioner
Extraordinary, for the management of those Affairs, as of the Moneys which he
remitted since his Return, by the Order of His Highness, and the Council.
To which is added a Particular of all other Receipts and Disbursements, of, or
upon those Moneys, to balance the whole Account.
Drawn up by the said Mr Morland, and published by the Special Order of His
Highness and the Council.
UPon the 20/30th of Nov. 1655. Master Pell and Master Morland His Highness
Commissioners Extraordinary for the Management of the Affairs of the poor
Protestants of the Valleys beyond sea, (and especially of the collected Moneys)
then residing at Geneva, by His Highness Orders, for that very end and purpose,
received a Letter written to them jointly, by the Right Honorable M. Secretary
Thurloe, bearing date the 08/18 Nov. 1655, wherein they were ordered to
distribute forthwith that part of the collected Moneys that had been remitted
into the hands of M. Lodwick Calandrine, Merchant of Geneva, an Extract of
which Letter is here inserted as follows:

Copy of Master Secr. Thurloes Letter to M. Pell, and M. Morland, bearing date
the 08/18 November. 1655.
Gentlemen,
HIs Highness and the Council, having taken into consideration what you have
represented concerning the condition of the poor persecuted Waldenses,
inhabiting the Valleys of Piemont, have resolved, that seven thousand five
hundred pounds sterling shall be distributed amongst them, for their present
relief; And there being five thousand pounds already in the hands of Master
Lodwick Calandrine, Merchant of Geneva, and two thousand five hundred pounds
more charged by Bill of Exchange a Month since, to be paid unto him; I have
writ unto him, to pay, as well the 5000 l. as the 2500 l. as you two shall
appoint: And His Highness pleasure is, That you cause a Distribution of the
said Money to be made amongst the poor Protestants, having respect therein,
(...) those of them, who are most necessitous, and have received the greatest
prejudice by the late Massacre and Persecution; wherein it is not possible for
Us here to give you particular Rules, but the doing thereof must be left to
your prudent Management: And for the residue of the Money collected upon this
occasion, further consideration will be had, to the end that it may be employed
for the most advantage; And you are to take such course in the Distribution of
the Money, That an Exact Account may be given, how it has been disposed of. I
rest

Your etc. Jo. Thurloe.


Upon the receipt of this Letter M. Pell and M. Morland immediately consulted
how they might in the best Manner execute these their Orders; And after they
had seriously considered thereof, they resolved (as they thought themselves in
duty obliged) to make a narrow search into the Way and Method how the former
sum of 2000 l. sterling, (which His Highness sent those poor people upon the
first news of their troubles) had been conveyed from Geneva into the Valleys;
That so, if they found that Method the most advantageous, they might follow the
same; but if not, That they might endeavor to find out a better.

Now upon a full enquiry, and after several discourses had with some, and
Informations from others, whom they judged the most fit to advise with in this
Affair, They thought it most expedient in that Conjuncture of time (for several
reasons which can not here conveniently be specified) to receive the said 7500
l. sterling. of M. Lodwick Calandrine, and compound with another (who also must be
nameless) for the speedy conveighance thereof into the Valleys, at his own
peril, (which neither M. Calandrine or any other would undertake at any rates)
for 926 French Livers.

Moneys received of Mr Calandrine by Mr Pell and Mr Morland.

Which Sum of 96428 Fr. Live. 11 Sols is the very sum which the Right
Honorable Commissioners in England agreed for with Major Chamberlain, to be
paid at Lions, as abovesaid.

Now Mr Pell and Mr Morland having received the abovesaid sum of 96428 Live.
11 Sols into their own hands, did immediately remit the same into the Valleys
for 926 Fr. Live. as abovesaid (as will appear in the sequel of this Chapt. by
the Original Acquittance of the poor people themselves.) But withal taking into
their serious considerations the urgent necessities of the poor people at that
time, having neither bread to eat, nor clothes to cover their nakedness, nor
indeed the least accommodation for a great multitude of sick, weak and aged
persons among them, did forthwith employ certain persons of known fidelity, and
who bore a real and undoubted affection toward the poor people and their
Cause, forthwith to lay out such a proportion of the 95598 Live. abovesaid, in
the buying of such quantities of Corn, Cloth, Bedding, Shoes, Linen and
Medicaments, as their necessities did then call for, and to distribute the same
to every family distinctly, according to the number of persons therein
contained.

Here (by the way) it will not be amiss to inform the Reader, that before this
7500 l. or any part thereof, was remitted into the hands of Mr. Pell and Mr.
Morland; That is to say, upon the first News of the Massacre, His Highness sent
the poor people upon his own private account, the sum of 2000 l. Sterling,
which was remitted by the Correspondents of Mr. Luke Lucy, at the rate of 56
English pence for a French Crown of 3 Livers; which 2000 l. Sterling after the
said rate, amount to the sum of 25486 Fr. Live. 12 Sols, and 6 Deniers, and was
moreover in the conveighance, by the advantage of the Species, augmented and
made 25510 Livers. Now forasmuch as this Sum was actually received by the poor
people, and laid out for their Supplies in the time of their extreme
Necessities, (as will appear by their own Acquittance, the Original whereof was
delivered up by Mr. Morland to the Right Honorable Commissioners) it will be
most convenient to prefix the same to the following Account, which will then
consist of 9500 l. Sterling.

It now remains only, that the Original and Authentic Acquittances of the
poor people themselves, be produced for the above-specified sum of 120182 Fr.
Live. for the value of 9500 l. Sterling; That is to say, the Remainder of 121938
Fr. Live. 11 Sols, after the abovesaid Deduction, first by M. Calandrine 830 F.
Live. 11 Sols, and afterward by M. Pell and M. Morland 926 F. Live. A true
Translat of which Acquittance is here inserted, the Original whereof was
delivered to the Right Honorable Commissioners by M. Morland, at his return
from Geneva into England.

Translat of the Original Acquittances of the Poor Protestants of the Valleys
of Piemont, made at S. Giovanni in the Valleys of Lucerna 14/24 August 1656.
WE the Deputies of the Reformed Churches of the Valleys of Piemont, do confess
to have received of the Moneys collected in England, for our relief, as follows:

First of all, We confess to have received through the hands of M. Calandrine
and other Merchants of Geneva, the sum of Twenty five thousand five hundred and
ten Fr. Livers, in the Month of July 1655. which came very seasonably for the
supplying our extreme necessities.

Item, We confess to have received of M. Pell and M. Morland, His Highness
Commissioners Extraordinary, in the Months of January and February 1656. the
sum of Ninety four thousand six hundred seventy two French Livers; the which
sum was employed and laid out in Coverings for Beds, Linen for Shifts, and
other Necessaries, in Corn for Seed, and present spending, in Medicaments, and
other Necessaries for sick people; and lastly for supplying the more urgent
necessities of several particular persons.

Both which sums abovesaid, have been remitted to us by the Order of His
Highness the Lord Protector, whose singular Zeal and Charity for the poor
distressed Members of Christ, which suffer for the Truth, we cannot
sufficiently admire; And as the Lord has raised Him as a most Illustrious
Example of Christian and Heroique Virtues, we beseech him from the very bottom
of our hearts, that he will be pleased likewise to make him an Example of his
extraordinary Blessing; And that he will also be pleased out of the abundant
Riches both of his spiritual and temporal Graces, reward the Churches of Great
Britain, who have been so touched with our Miseries, and with so much pity and
compassion refreshed our Bowels, that so we may have always matter of rejoicing
and blessing his holy Name for the welfare and prosperity of that Commonwealth.
Even so Amen.

In testimony whereof we have subscribed the 14 August 1656.

The true Original of this Acquittance (being subscribed by several persons,
whose Names it is thought fit for several weighty reasons to conceal) was
delivered by M. Morland into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners,
appointed by His Highness and the Council for the Management of those Affairs.

Thus were the Bowels of those poor Creatures exceedingly refreshed for a
season. But alas it was not long before they had need of fresh Supplies, for
they had neither any certain habitation, nor any opportunity or means to till
their Lands, or to traffic for their future subsistence, being altogether
destitute of Cattle and all other Materials and Utensils, insomuch that
whatsoever was distributed amongst them, served but for very little more then
from hand to mouth, and to supply their present necessities. The plain truth
is, the condition of those poor Creatures was then (and God knows is to this
very day) very sad and lamentable. On the one side, what was then given them,
did only serve to fortify Nature to be more sensible of Hunger, Cold, and the
Miseries of this Life, and to enable them to cry for more relief; and on the
other side, to have given them greater Sums at a time, would have most
certainly exposed them to the fury of those ravenous evening Wolves, who would
much sooner, and with sevenfold more greediness have set upon, and devoured
those poor Lambs, had they had but golden fleeces upon their Backs.

Wherefore it was then Resolved (as the securest way) by His Highness and the
Council, That the poor people might be supplied from time to time, so as they
might subsist, until Providence should open a way for the obtaining for them
some kind of settlement and re-establishment.

By this the Reader may easily conjecture, how soon there was like to be
further occasion of soliciting His Highness and the Council for new supplies,
the which Mr Morland accordingly from time to time did, that Affair being now
wholly committed to his charge alone; and Mr Pell (according to His Highness
Orders) being now returned to his former employment of Residency at Zurick.

And having an opportunity in the Interval of time, to consider the
inconveniences of the former Remission of the collected Moneys from England to
Geneva, especially as to the dear rates, and also the great distance of time it
had upon the way (which notwithstanding as Affairs then stood, when the said
Moneys were remitted, could not easily be prevented, though the Right Honorable
Committee used therein their utmost endeavors) whereas the poor people had
present and urgent occasion for Moneys; And forasmuch as it was not at that
time altogether improbable that they might have been set upon on a sudden, and
so have perished for want of speedy relief, the said Mr Morland began to inform
himself, in order to the finding out a more speedy and profitable way for the
conveighance of the said Moneys for the future; And at last he met with a
certain person, who made him a Proposition as follows; Namely,

That for 2 per 100 he would undertake to advance any sum or sums of Money
in any of those parts, in case Mr. Morland would assure him, that he should be
repaid at London; And all this according to the ordinary rate of Exchange of
Moneys from Paris to London; And so by consequence any sum of Moneys might
upon any occasion be remitted from London to the Valleys in the space of 15 or
16 days, and all for 2 per 100.

This Proposition Mr Morland communicated into England, and received an Answer
to it of the 7 Febr. 5/6; an Extract whereof is here inserted, viz.

Extract of the R. Honorable Mr Secretary Thurloe's Letter of the 7 Febr.
1655/6;.
SIR,
I Have communicated your Expedient for returning Moneys, and I have sent you
the enclosed Papers, which you are to consider; And if you can contract to take
up Moneys so as these Papers state it, you are to make sure of 7000 l. sterling.
And you may charge the Bills for it upon Sir Thomas Viner and Sir Christopher
Pack, Aldermen of the City of London, and they will be duly paid: But be sure
you make your Bargain safe and with good advice. And also how to dispose of the
Money (which must be ready at Geneva) for which you shall have Order afterward.

Jo. Thurloe.


Upon the receipt of this, and after a mature consideration, Mr Morland made an
Accord with the person abovesaid in the following terms.

A Translat of the Accord made between Samuel Morland Esquire His Highness
Commissioner Extraordinary on the one part, and J.N. on the other, for the
remitting of 7000 l. from England to Geneva.
I Whose name is here underwritten, out of Consideration had to the poor
people of Piemont, have contracted with Sam. Morland Esquire His Highness
Commissioner Extraordinary for the sum of seven thousand pounds sterling, for
the which shall be furnished me Bills of Exchange payable to J. N. and J. S. or
according to their Order, at first sight, at the rate of 54 pence and 3
farthings for every crown of 3 Livers Tournois. For the which I hereby promise
to pay the value to the said Mr. Morland by advance, in Spanish Pistols, or
French gold Pistols at 10 Livers 10 Sols the Pistol, and French Louis or
Crowns, at 3 Livers the Crown; The said Mr. Morland promising me on his part,
that the said Bills of Exchange which he shall deliver me, shall be duly paid
me; As also making good to me two in the hundred, which shall be in
consideration both of advancing the Moneys here, and also for the Charges it
will occasion me, and the hazard I run, together with all sorts of Provisions.

And besides this I promise, That in case the Exchange from Paris to London
come to be abated within the space of 15 days lower then the said price of 54
pence per crown, to make the same good to Mr. Morland, for the benefit of the
poor people: And on the contrary, in case it come to be higher, he shall make
the same good to me. Forasmuch as I pretend nothing more or less then the said
2 per 100 for the advance of the Moneys, the provision, and the hazard which I
run. In witness whereof we have signed this present Accord, in two Copies of
the same Tenor.

Made at Geneva. 27 Febr. 1656. In the presence of several persons whose names
it is thought fit to conceal, S. Morland. J. N.

Upon the signing of this Accord, there was the value of 7000 l. Sterling as
abovesaid, actually paid M. Morland, and the Bills of Exchange afterward duly
honored by Sir Thomas Vyner and Sir Christopher Pack in London.

The first of April M. Morland received a Letter from M. Secretary Thurloe, in
answer to his of the 26 Feb. and 9 March (wherein he had represented the sad
condition of the poor people at that time.)

Extract of the Right Honorable Mr. Secretary Thurloes Letter to Mr. Morland of
the 20 March 1656.
OF the Seven thousand pounds Sterling paid unto you, you are forthwith to
cause Two thousand pound to be distributed amongst the poor Protestants of the
Valleys, for the relief of their Necessities, taking care that it be done
justly, and with the best advantage for their good, and giving Account thereof
hither with what speed may be.

Jo. Thurloe.


Here it will be necessary to advertise the Reader, that at that time when Mr.
Morland took up the abovesaid 7000 l. at Geneva, he foresaw that there was like
to be some distance of time between that of his taking up the Moneys, and the
Receipt of his Orders for the Distribution thereof, which made him desirous to
improve the said Moneys for the best advantage, by putting it out to profit,
yet with this caution, That he put it out first into safe hands: and secondly,
that he might recall the same, if need were, at a days warning, or at two at
the most; All which he did, and by that means when he received his Orders for
the distribution of the 2000 l. abovesaid amongst the poor people, in stead of
2000 l. he sent 2091 l. 12 s. 6 d. (as will appear by their Original
Acquittance) and yet for the 5000 l. remaining, he had left the value of 5010
l. 14 s. 9 d. as will be more clear by the following Account.

It will be here likewise necessary to advertise the Reader for his better
satisfaction, That after Mr. Morland had received his Orders for the
Distribution of the abovesaid 2000 l. he was forced by reason of the great
danger there was of loss upon the Species in that conjuncture of time, together
with the Stratagems of their Adversaries, who knowing that there was Money
coming to them from England and other parts, sought all means to procure them
great loss upon their Moneys, by obtaining Edicts from the Court of Savoy for
that purpose; to send an Express into the Valleys to inform himself punctually
what Species would be most advantegeous; and so there was a Months time lost
or more, before he could have the poor peoples Acquittance for the Receipt of
the Moneys.

A Translat of the Original Acquittance of the poor People of the Valleys for
2091 l. 12. s. 6 d. which was delivered into their hands the 29th of May 1656.
WE the Deputies of the Reformed Churches of the Valleys of Piemont,
acknowledge to have received of M. Morland His Highness Commissioner
Extraordinary, the sum of Twenty seven thousand five hundred and six French
Livers, and ten Sols, or Two thousand and ninety one pounds twelve shillings
six pence sterling, being a part of the Collected Moneys remitted from England,
by Order of His Hsghness The Lord Protector; The which sum of Twenty seven
thousand five hundred and six French Livers, ten Sols, was immediately
distributed to each particular Church, according to the propositioms heretofore
established by Mr. Pell and Mr. Morland, with respect had to their Sufferings
more or less by the late unhappy War and Persecution raised against them for
the cause and sake of their Religion, in the Year 1655. to the end that every
one might receive his just portion, and by that means they might in some
measure be relieved according to their present necessities; for the which we
join in returning hearty Thanks to the Authors of this charitable Benevolence,
beseeching the Lord to pour down upon them abundantly, as a Reward of their
Christian Zeal, both the Blessings of this life and that which is to come.

Made the 29 May 1656.
The true Original of this Acquittance (being subscribed by several persons,
whose Names it is thought fit for several weighty reasons to conceal) was
delivered by M. Morland into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners,
appointed by His Highness and the Council for the Management of those Affairs.

Mr. Morland after the Distribution of the abovesaid 2091 l. 12 s. 6 d. having
a better opportunity and more leisure to perfect the way and method of the
Distribution of the Collected Moneys, among the poor Protestants, after advice
had with those whom he thought most fit to counsel him in this matter, at last
pitched upon certain Instructions, which he caused afterward to be observed
exactly in the Distribution of the remainder of the 7000 l. Sterling, and after
that of the last 2000 l. Sterling, which he remitted since his return. An
Extract of which Instructions (so far as they are fit to be communicated) are
here inserted.

An Extract of the Instructions given by Mr. Morland, to those whom he
entrusted for the Distribution of the collected Moneys among the poor people of
the Valleys of Piemont.
General Articles as touching the Distribution.
I. THose who have any Estate, be it in movables or otherwise, ought not to
expect a Gratuity proportionable to their losses; For by this means those who
yet remained in some tolerable posture of Subsistence after the Invasion, would
devour the whole, or at least the greatest part of that which ought to relieve
those who are in most distress.

II. Those who had almost all their Patrimony in Lands, ought to consider that
the exemption from Taxes which was granted them, could do no less then ease
them of a considerable part of their losses, and they ought by no means now to
pretend to the same proportion with those who had little or no movables, and
have beside that been totally ruined.

The more particular Rules or Articles.
1. According to the general Rules abovesaid, they shall proceed more
particularly, and distinguish those who are the most necessitous at present
into these three following Ranks: 1. Those, who had very little or nothing at
all before the Massacre. 2. Those, who had enough tolerably to subsist. 3.
Those, who had some kind of abundance.


And thus there shall be established three Classes or Orders, namely, a Lower,
a Middle, and a Higher, placing the first sort in the lowest Rank, the second
sort in the middle Rank, and the last sort in the highest Rank or Classis;
provided still that there be no departing from the General Rules
above-specified, and that all possible equity and due proportion be observed
amongst the several families thus ranged.

2. Touching the Ministers.
The Ministers shall not pretend any thing of Ordinary Salary or Subsistence
from these collected Moneys, because they have been paid otherwise by their
respective Churches, Nevertheless they shall be comprised in the Distribution
for the reparation of losses, in the same Proposition with others.

3. Those who have been absent and had their Subsistence in other Countries.
Those who have been absent in the time of the Distribution, and had their
Subsistence in other Countries, at the same time, shall not pretend any thing
from the Distributions heretofore administered.

4. Those who have been heretofore administered to in the time of their Sickness.
The sick, who have been heretofore relieved in their sickness with the
public Moneys, shall pretend nothing from the former Distributions made,
forasmuch as they have already received more then their portion in the time of
their necessity.

5. Those who have been forgotten by neglect.
Those who have been heretofore forgotten by neglect or otherwise in the time
of the said Distributions, shall receive at present that which was then their
portion: provided that there be no other reason of this their not having a
divident heretofore, then a bare and simple forgetfulness and neglect of them.

6. The Wounded.
The wounded persons shall be distinguished, into those who have been maimed,
and thereby rendered altogether useless; and those who have in some manner
recovered the use of their Limbs. To the first there shall be an especial
regard had; and the last shall also be ranked in the first and best Classis,
provided that there be good Evidence of the damage which they have suffered.

7. Those who are indebted to the Physician in particular.
Those who owe any thing to the Physician in particular, shall pay the
Physician out of their own, because there has almost always been a public
Physician provided; And this would otherwise be a door opened to a very great
Confusion.

8. Those who have received more then their due in the former Distributions.
Those who have received more then their due portion in the former
Distributions, shall make restitution by a deduction of so much of their
portion in the present Distribution.

9. Those who have received any thing out of the time of Distribution.
That which has been formerly given to the poor out of the time of
Distribution, shall not be accounted to them in the present Distribution; But
if there be found any, who had competent Estates, who thus received any Moneys,
it shall be accounted to them. And the Commissioners are to take all possible
care therein, that this Article be observed with all equity and uprightness.

10. The Arrears of the Ministers.
Those Ministers, to whom any thing was formerly due before the Massacre, shall
make their address to their several Churches for payment thereof, and not
demand it of the Public.

11. Their Physician.
Their Physician shall be paid out of the Public, according to the former
agreement made with him. And for the time to come the people of the Valleys
shall compound with him for a certain stipend; provided that he engage to
reside always among them; And for the surplus of his maintenance he shall be
paid for his pains in a moderate way by those particulars who shall employ him.

12. The Apothecaries and Chirurgions.
There shall be strict Examination had into what the Apothecaries and
Chirurgions have provided formerly for those poor people, who are not now able
to repay them, and they shall be satisfied out of the Public. But as for
those who have wherewithal, they shall repay the said Apothecaries and
Chirurgions out of their own, and not demand it from the Public.

13. Those who have been most active for the Defense of their Country.
Those who pretend Gratifications for their particular Service that they have
done for their Country, in exposing their persons to great dangers, and by
enduring Extraordinary hardship, ought to content themselves with this
recompense, namely, the good Testimony that they have in their own Consciences
to have done worthily, together with the praises of all honest people, as
likewise that they have their portion equal with the rest in the Distribution.

14. The Losses caused by the War.
There shall be particular regard had to those losses, which have been caused
by the War, as pillaging, burning, and the like; provided it plainly appear
that it has proceeded from the War, and not from the storms of Thunder, Hail,
or other accidents.

15. The Alms of Bread, Wine, etc.
Those who have furnished the poor with Bread or Wine in the time of their
former desolations, shall not now demand a reward or recompense for the same,
by reason of the great confusion that would arise from thence; As likewise it
would be a shame to bring their charitable actions upon a public Account.

Given at Geneva 1 June 1656 by S. Morland.

THere were several other particulars of consequence in the said Instructions,
which (for several weighty Reasons) it is thought most convenient rather to
pass over, then communicate in a public way; however these may be sufficient
to give the sober and understanding Reader a taste and relish of the whole; and
what ear has been taken in the Management of that Affair. And indeed all that
could then be done was but little enough to preserve those distressed Creatures
from perishing for want of the necessary accommodations of this life.

It was not many Weeks after the Distribution of the 2091 l. -- 12 s. -- 06 d.
-- sterling, before the poor peoples necessities began to be renewed. True it
is, they did endeavor to the utmost to conceal their wants, having so lately
received an Alms. But however it was in a short space of time too visible, how
^eer diverse of them were to being starved to death, or otherwise perishing in a
miserable manner. Whereupon Mr Morland gave speedy notice thereof to His
Highness and the Council, who immediately made an Order which he received the
26 of August, in a Letter from Mr Secretary Thurloe to the same purpose.

Extract of Mr. Secretary Thurloes Letter of the 14 Aug. 1656.
YOu will receive herewith the Order of His Highness and the Council for
distributing the 5000 l. amongst the poor Protestants of Piemont, wherein I
desire you as to use your wonted care and circumspection, so to dispatch this
with what speed you may; In respect I find a necessity for your return as soon
as you have finished the Distribution of this 5000 l.

Jo. Thurloe.


A Copy of the Orde of His Highness and the Council. Thursday 14 Aug. 1656. At
the Council at Whitehall.
ORdered by His Highness the Lord Protector and the Council, That the 5000 l.
of the collected Moneys for the distressed Protestants of the Valleys of
Piemont, which was formerly returned over into the hands of Mr Morland, and now
remaining with him, be distributed among the said Protestants by Mr Morland, in
the same sort as the former sums of Money have been distributed amongst them.

Henry Scobell Clerk of the Council.

Mr Morland having received these Orders, forthwith employed his utmost
endeavors for the speedy Execution of the same; But forasmuch as the said sum
of 5010 l. 14. s. 9. d. sterling. or 65894 French Livers 10 Sols, was all in white
Money (as by the foregoing Account appears) not only the portage thereof was
much more chargeable, but also the trouble of changing some part thereof into
gold (which was then absolutely necessary) took up a great space of time, so
that it could not be conveyed so speedily as otherwise it might have been
into the Valleys; Nor when it was there, could the whole sum of 65894 French
Livers be so soon distributed as before, by reason that those who were
entrusted in the business, were but newly chosen, and therefore were to examine
with all possible exactness the Accounts of those who had been before entrusted
in the same business, and also to rectify all miscarriages or inconveniences
they should find to have been in the management of the Distribution of former
Sums; And all this before they could with any Method or Order proceed to
distribute any part of this Sum. Besides that, this was to be as a leading
Card, and a Rule for the constant measure of all future Distributions.

However on the 14 Jan. 1656/7 the abovesaid Sum of 5010 l. 14. s. 9. d. or
65340 Fr. Livers, was actually distributed amongst the poor people, as will
appear by the following Original Acquittance, signed by their own hands.

A Translat of the Original Acquittance of the poor People of the Valleys of
Piemont, for the 65340 Fr. Livers remitted to them by Mr Morland.
WE the Deputies of the Reformed Churches of the Valleys of Piemont,
acknowledge to have received of Mr Morland, Commissioner Extraordinary of His
most serene Highness, The Lord Protector of Great Britain, The Sum of Sixty
five thousand three hundred and forty French Livers, arising out of the
Collections made in Great Britain for our relief. For the which Sum we acquit
and discharge the said Mr Morland, returning him our hearty thanks for the care
he has taken of us, in the procuring and remitting the same to us; and above
all, The Charitable Givers, for the Compassion they have had of our pitiful
estate.

Made the 14 of January, 1656/7.
The true Original of this Acquittance (being subscribed by several persons,
whose Names it is thought fit for several weighty reasons to conceal) was
delivered by M. Morland into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners,
appointed by His Highness and the Council for the Management of those Affairs.

About the 20. November. 56/7 Mr Morland received Orders in a Letter from Mr
Secretary Thurloe for his return into England, where he arrived the 20 December.
56/7.

Upon his arrival here, he gave an Account to His Highness and the Council of
his management of the whole Affair, and likewise of the sad condition the
people were then in. And not many weeks after, having received intelligence
that they stood in need of fresh supplies, he solicited in their behalf, and
upon the 27 of Jan. 1656. an Order was granted as follows: Copy of the Order of
His Highness and the Council, for the remitting of 2000 l. sterling. into the
Valleys of Piemont. Tuesday 27 January 1656. At the Council at Whitehall.
Whereas the Committee for the Protestants in the Valleys of Piemont have
offered it to His Highness and the Council as their humble opinion, That two
thousand pounds of the Collection-money for the said Protestants be speedily
remitted beyond sea for their present relief; Ordered by His Highness the Lord
Protector and the Council, That Sr Thomas Vyner and Sr Christopher Pack,
Knights, Treasurers for the said Collection-moneys, do, out of the said Moneys
remaining in their hands, forthwith pay two Thousand pounds to Mr Morland, who
is to take care for remitting the same in the most safe and convenient way, for
the said Protestants present relief.

Hen. Scobell Clerk of the Council.



Now Mr Morland, though he received this Order from His Highness and the
Council, yet forasmuch as he had no particular Instructions concerning the
remitting any Sums for the future, before his departure from Geneva, he durst
not engage to remit the Moneys before he had first wrote to the party who
remitted the former Sums, to know his mind, and so to prevent all
difficulties; and so it was a Month after the receipt of his Orders, before he
could give punctual directions to his Correspondents beyond sea, for the
remission of it. But so soon as ever he had received an answer from beyond sea,
he forthwith gave such Instructions as were necessary, and on the 5th of April,
1657. the poor people actually received the Moneys, as appears by their
following formal Acquittance.

A Translat of the Original Acquittance of the poor people of the Valleys of
Plemont, for the Value of the last 2000 l. sterling.
WE, the Deputies of the Reformed Churches of the Valleys of Piemont, confess
to have received of Mr Morland the Sum of Twenty six Thousand one hundred and
twenty six Livers; The which sum was distributed amongst all our Churches, that
is to say, to every one according to their losses and most urgent necessities,
conformable to the abovesaid Mr Morland's former Instructions for that purpose;
And as the said sum is a part of the Collections made in England, we return our
most humble thanks to His Highness The Lord Protector, and pray God for the
prosperity of His Person, and of the whole Church and flourishing Commonwealth
of Great Britain.

Made the 5th of April, 1657.
The true Original of this Acquittance, being subscribed by several persons,
(whose names it is thought fit for several weighty reasons to conceal) was
delivered by Mr Morland into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners,
appointed by His Highness and the Council for the Management of those Affairs.

About three Months after the distribution of the 2000 l. abovesaid, Mr
Morland, having received certain Intelligence of many new grievances and
vexations which those poor people then lay under, by reason of the
extraordinary hatred and malice of their popish Adversaries, communicated the
same to the Right Honorable Commissioners appointed by His Highness and the
Council for the Management of those Affairs, who immediately represented the
same to Mr Secretary Thurloe, and he to His Highness and the Council, and
thereupon there was an Order issued out as follows:

Tuesday 28 July, 1657. At the Council at Whitehall.
Whereas the Committee for the Valleys of Piemont have by their Order of the
22th of July instant, offered it to His Highness and the Council as their
humble advice, That one thousand pounds may be forthwith transmitted as
formerly, for relief of the poor Protestants in the Valleys of Piemont; Ordered
by His Highness The Lord Protector and the Council, That Sr Thomas Vyner, and
Sr Christopher Pack Knights, Treasurers for the Moneys collected for Relief of
the said Protestants, do out of the Moneys remaining in their hands of the said
Collections, forthwith pay One thousand pounds to Mr Morland, who is to take
care for remitting the same, in the most safe and convenient way, for the said
Protestants present support.

W. Jessop Clerk of the Council.

Mr Morland, upon the receipt of the abovesaid Order, did his utmost endeavor
to have remitted and sent over the Moneys the same week; but it so falling out,
that at that very time the great abundance of violent rains that fell in those
parts, caused such mighty floods that there was no passage, for at least a
month or six weeks space into those Valleys, the remission was by that means
much retarded; however upon the 24h of October. 1657. it was actually received by
the poor people, as appears by their following Authentic Acquittance;

A Translat of the Original Acquittance of the poor People of the Valleys of
Piemont, for the last 1000 l. remitted to them by M. Morland.
WE the Deputies, etc. of the Reformed Churches in the Valleys of Piemont,
confess to have actually and really received of Mr. Morland, the sum of
Thirteen thousand and three Livers, and thirteen Sols Turnois, as part of the
charitable Benevolence of our beloved and honored Brethren in Jesus Christ, of
Great Britain; And this besides and over and above all other Sums which have
been remitted formerly into our hands, the which Sum of Thirteen thousand three
Livers, and thirteen Sols Turnois, has been distributed amongst all our
Churches in a just proportion, and with a due respect to the number of persons,
the greatest sufferers, and their present more urgent necessities; For the
which we return the Donors all the Thanks we are able for their holy and
exemplary Charity toward us, beseeching from the bottom of our hearts the
Father of Mercies to be their abundant Reward.

Made the 24 of October 1657.
The true Original of this Acquittance, being subscribed by several persons
(whose names it is thought fit for several weighty reasons to conceal) was
delivered by Mr Morland into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners,
appointed by His Highness and the Council for the Management of those Affairs.

This very Month was not many days expired, before Mr. Morland received
Intelligence of the renewed Wants and Exigences of those poor People, which he
immediately communicated to the Right Honorable Mr. Secretary Thurloe, and Mr.
Secretary again to His Highness and the Council, who forthwith caused a Meeting
of the Commissioners, and upon further advice with them, granted an Order as
follows:

Monday 25th November 1657. At the Council at White Hall.
ORdered by His Highness the Lord Protector and the Council, That over and
above the sums of money already transmitted to the poor Protestants of Piemont,
out of the Moneys collected for them, there be the sum of Fifteen hundred
pounds more forthwith sent unto them for their relief; And that the Committee
for the Affairs of the said Protestants do take care the same be sent
accordingly, and that the Treasurers be allowed 2 l. per Cent. for the charge
of transmitting the same upon their Account.

W. Jessop Clerk of the Council.

Mr. Morland upon the receipt of this Order, by the direction of the Right
Honorable Commissioners, remitted over the said Sum of 1500 l. in the same
manner and method as he had remitted the former Sums, and accordingly has
received certain advice of the safe arrival thereof in those parts; but by
reason of the incredible quantities of snow which have lain ever since upon
those Mountains, which have stopped up all passages of late, the poor peoples
Original Acquittance is not yet come to his hands, for the actual Distribution
thereof; moreover he daily expects the same, and having received it, shall
deliver it into the hands of the Right Honorable Commissioners.

It will be necessary in the close of this Account, to advertise the Readers,
that besides what was remitted over from England into the Valleys, there were
several smaller Sums given out to some particular persons here in England,
under the notion of eminent Sufferers for and in that Cause, and that upon
pretended Recommendations from the poor people themselves and their Deputies,
which they then presented to the Right Honorable Commissioners during Mr.
Morlands abode at Geneva; but two of them, by name Captain Raymond and
Christiana Vlrick, having most wickedly and treacherously counterfeited those
their said Attestations and other papers (as it afterward plainly appeared
when it was too late upon Mr. Morlands return into England, who by the Order of
the Right Honorable Commissioners made a further search into that business) it
is thought fit that those their unworthy actions be made manifest to all men.
The first of them immediately upon Mr. Morlands return, having a guilty
conscience, fled beyond sea; and the last upon Examination, confessed to one of
the Right Honorable Commissioners, that she had most wretchedly suffered her
self to be tempted, and prevailed upon by the devil, to the commission of that
so heinous and foul a sin.

This is now the sum and substance of the Account which Mr. Morland has in
part formerly, and the rest of late delivered into the hands of the Right
Honorable Commissioners, appointed by His Highness and the Council for the
Management of those Affairs; But for the ease and better satisfaction of all
those that desire more fully to inform themselves herein, and that they may as
it were with one glance of their eye, have a full prospect of all the Receipts
and Disbursements of or upon the said Collected Moneys, as well at home as
abroad, they are desired to peruse the following General Account.

S. Morland.

As for the Moneys that yet remain in the hands of the Treasurers, the good
People of this Nation are desired to believe and be assured, that it has
hitherto been His Highness exceeding great Care that no part of the Collected
Moneys might be distributed or delivered out, but by the advice, and through
the hands of persons of known Honor and approved Fidelity; so likewise it shall
be His constant endeavor, that what yet remains, or shall hereafter be
collected and laid up in the hands of the Treasurers (who are as ready now as
ever, to receive what shall be further collected) shall be improved for the
best advantage of those for whom it was (or shall be) solely intended. For
which end and purpose His Highness, after mature Consultation had both with His
Commissioners at home, and His public Ministers abroad, has already caused
some part thereof to be put out to Interest in sure hands (but so, that it may
be called in upon urgent occasion) and for the future will take such
Resolutions as the Necessities of those poor People, and the Circumstances of
their Condition shall require, which (the Lord knows) is now as sad as ever in
many respects, and without the same miraculous hand of Providence that has
hitherto preserved them, must in a short time inevitably perish. Wherefore if
there be any charitable Souls who have yet contributed nothing to the relief of
their poor distressed Brethren, let them please to peruse the following
Discourse, and it may be they may find there such Arguments as may awaken
them, and quicken their hearts to the performance of so good and Christian a
Work.

A Brief Account of the present State and Condition of the poor Protestants in
the Valleys of Piemont: Together with A Serious Exhortation to all those who
have hitherto contributed nothing toward the Relief of their distressed
Brethren, that if the Lord so please, their hearts may be yet moved to the
performance of so good and charitable a work.
By SAM. MORLAND.
Prove. 24.11, 12. 11 If thou forbear to deliver them that are drawn unto death,
and those that are ready to be slain,

12 If thou say, Behold we knew it not; does not he that pondereth the heart,
consider it? and he that keeps thy soul, does not he know it? and shall not
he render to every man according to his work?


IF Words in season be (or at ^ast ought to be) acceptable things, then I trust
I may, without offense, here in the close of this Narrative, entreat all those
who have hitherto contributed nothing toward the relief and support of their
poor distressed Brethren, to suffer a word of serious Exhortation, as hoping
(if the Lord so please) it may have such blessed effects upon their spirits,
that they may at last both cheerfully partake in the work, and hereafter have a
suitable share in the reward.

The plain truth is, it may be righteously presumed from what has been already
said, that all sober and ingenuous persons will be so far from reserving in
their minds the least scruple as touching the honest and faithful
Administration of the collected Moneys, that on the contrary all those who have
contributed freely, will have much satisfaction in what they have already done,
and be the more ready to do the like for the future, as there shall be
occasion; and those who have either come off coldly, or given nothing at all,
will account it as a singular mercy of God toward them to have such an
opportunity as this is, afforded them, of redeeming the time, and of making
recompense for their former backwardness, by drawing out now at last a larger
portion of their love, and by doubling the effects of their charity and tender
compassions toward the relieving of their distressed fellow members.

And indeed, have they but a heart to the work, I shall soon lead them where
they shall even smite upon their breasts, and their hearts shall even melt
within them, to hear the lamentable and woeful cries of above ten Thousand
souls, who are at this very day sitting among the potsherds, with sackcloth and
ashes spread under them, and lifting up their voice with weeping, in the words
of Job, when he was now in the depth of his misery and anguish, Have pity on
us, have pity on us, O ye our friends, for the hand of God has touched us!

If there be any who are curious to know who these men are, and what was their
Fathers house, I shall endeavor to satisfy them in a few words at present,
referring them for a larger Discourse upon that subject, to the History of the
Evangelical Churches of the Valleys of Piemont, which will, God willing,
speedily present it self to public view.

These poor creatures are the children and offspring of those ancient
Inhabitants of the Valleys of Piemont, who have born testimony to the truth in
the purity thereof, ever since the days of Christ and his Apostles; And though
they have been ofttimes furiously assaulted by the sons of violence, yet God
has always by a miraculous providence preserved those little sparks in the
greatest floods of persecutions, that either the subtilty or malice of their
enemies could ever raise against them, according to that excellent Character
the learned Beza gives of them in his Treatise of the famous Pillars of
Learning and Religion. Icones Theod Be^ae de Valdens.
Valdenses liceat mihi Veteris Christianae purioris Ecclesiae semen vocare, ut
pote quos constet vere admirabili Dei Providentia; neque illis infinitis
tempestatibus, quibus est per tot secula Christianus Orbis concussus, and
occidens tandem a Pseud-episcopo Romano misere oppressus, neque adversus illos
proprie excitatis horribilibus persecutionibus eo adduci potuissent, ut
Idolomaniae, and Romanae tyrannidi assentirentur, i. e. As for the Waldenses,
give me leave to style them the very seed of the Primitive and purer Christian
Church, as those who have been manifestly upheld by the admirable Providence of
God; so that neither those infinite storms and tempests, whereby the whole
Christian world has been shaken for so many Ages together, and at length the
Western parts so miserably oppressed by the Bishop of Rome, falsely so called; nor
those horrible persecutions, which have been directly raised against them, were
ever able so far to prevail upon them, as to make them yield a voluntary
subjection to the Romish Tyranny and Idolatry.

Yea there are diverse sober and learned men in the neighboring and adjacent
parts, who confidently assert from some late discoveries, That this was the
very desert whither the woman fled when she was persecuted by the Dragon with
seven heads and ten horns. Revel. 12.6. Cant. 2.14.
And that it was in the Clefts of these very Rocks, and in the secret places of
the stairs of these very Valleys, that the Dove of Christ remained in those
dark and gloomy seasons of the 8, 9 and 10 Centuries, at which time also the
Italian foxes began to spoil the Vines with the tender grapes, Cant. 2.15.

These things I have the rather hinted, (though briefly) to signify how
precious these people have always been in the eyes of the great God of heaven
and earth, and consequently to heighten our love and esteem of the scattered
remnant of their seed, of whom a considerable number have been most cruelly
murdered and massacred, within these few late years, and whose souls are now
under the Altar, waiting and expecting till God avenge their blood upon them
that dwell upon the earth: Some of their Virgins ravished, and afterward
abused in so horrid a manner as is not fit to be mentioned, till such time as
they gave up the ghost in unspeakable pain and anguish! The brains of innocent
and tender infants sodden and eaten by their murderers! The flesh of some
plucked off their bones (while they were yet alive) till they were become mere
Skeletons or Anatomies! Many feeble, sick and aged persons of 90, and 100 years
most inhumanely burnt in their beds! others most barbarously treated without
any respect to their hoary heads or gray hairs! with a multitude of other
strange, and almost unheard of cruelties, which would be too long here to
rehearse.

Now as for those of them that survived and escaped the rage of those men of
blood (which are the people I now recommend to your Christian charity and
pity) alas! they were then immediately exposed to the extremities of hunger,
cold and nakedness, and almost all the miseries of this life; being forced to
wander up and down upon the craggy Rocks and snowy Mountains, in a most
desolate and forlorn condition; yea and even to this present hour they hunger,
and thirst, and are naked, and buffeted, and have no certain dwelling place, 1
Cor. 4.11. To this very day, they labor under most heavy burdens which are
laid upon their shoulders by those rigid Taskmasters of the Church of Rome. To
this very day the enemies of the Truth plough and make long furrows upon the
backs of those poor creatures, By forbidding them all manner of traffic or
commerce for their sustenance; By robbing them of their goods and Estates; By
driving them in a most savage manner from their ancient houses and habitations,
and forcing them to sell their birthright for a mess of pottage; By banishing
their Ministers, who are the shepherds of the flocks, that so the wolves may
the better come in, and devour the sheep; By ravishing their young women and
maidens; yea by murdering many innocent souls, as they pass along the
highways about their private occasions; By cruel mockings and revilings; By
continual menacings and threats of another Massacre, if possible, sevenfold
more bloody then the former: What shall I say? Those very Valleys, where they
inhabit at this day, are (...) the indefatigable industry of their Popish
adversaries made no other then a prison o^ ^^^geon, to which the Fort of La
Torre serves as a door, where they may let in (when they please) a troop of
murderers, utterly to destroy and exterminate those poor silly wretches. To all
this I must add, (which indeed is most of all considerable as to the drawing
forth your hearts to contribute toward their present relief) that
notwithstanding all those large supplies, which have been hitherto sent them,
either from England or Foreign States, yet so great is the number of those
hungry creatures, and so grievous are the oppressions of their Popish enemies,
who lay in wait to bereave them of whatsoever is given them, and snatch at
every morsel of meat that goes into their mouths, that verily, some of them are
at this very day ready ever and anon to eat their own flesh for want of bread.
The tongue of the suckling is ready to cleave to the roof of his mouth, and the
young children ask bread, and no man gives it unto them: And in many places the
young and the old lie on the ground in the streets.

In sum, Curae l^ves loquuntur, ingentes stupent. Senec. Trag. in Hippolyt.

Their miseries are more sad and grievous then words can express; and they are
in a manner dying while they yet live: No grapes in their vineyards: No cattle
in their fields: No herds in their stals: No corn in their gardners: No meal in
their barrel: No oil in their cruse: The stock which was gathered for them by
the good people of this Nation, wasting apace, and when that is once spent,
they must inevitably perish, except you are now pleased to lend your helping
hand as other good Christians before you have done, and so make such an
addition to that Sum which yet remains, that the Interest thereof may in some
manner give them a being and subsistence in the world, and so the Capital remain
entire, to be ready upon any occasion (which God of his mercy prevent) in case
those poor creatures should meet with fresh assaults from their Popish enemies.
And all till such time as Providence shall find out some way for their
settlement and re-establishment.

True it is, I cannot but expect that some covetous, or ill-affected to the
cause of Christ and his people, should here step in, and throw on the cold
water of self-interest, and carnal objections, to quench if possible the fire
of your Christian zeal. I say, some will certainly here object, and say, But if
there be so many thousands remaining, which are yet undistributed, what need is
there (at least for the present) of any further supplies?

I answer,

1. Though there are several Thousands of pounds remaining, yet there are also
several Thousands of those poor creatures to be daily fed, clothed and
sheltered with it; and ten thousand times ten thousand things they still stand
in need of.

2. God alone knows how far off may yet be the time of their re-establishment
in their ancient habitations and privileges: And if so, so soon as ever the
stock they have at present is once spent, their case will be sevenfold more
lamentable and desperate then before.

3. This is now the second opportunity the Lord has in mercy given you of
relieving his poor Saints; and let me tell you, whoever ye are that shut up
your bowels now, it may be a third opportunity may never be afforded you. Those
poor wretches may possibly be taken out of the miseries of this life, and you
in judgment have the same afflictions or worse befall you; yea, the time may
come, when your own Consciences may sadly reflect upon you as guilty, in some
sense, of the blood of these poor men, and of a tacit compliance with their
murderers, there being no so great difference between the hard-heartedness of
him that finding his neighbor in the ditch, offers not to help him out, and the
cruelty of the man that first threw him in. Wherefore if you now forbear to
deliver them that are drawn unto death, and those that are ready to be slain;
If you say, Behold we know it not; does not he that pondereth the heart,
consider it? and he that keeps your souls, does not he know it? and shall not
he render to every man according to his works?

If therefore so many thousand distressed souls be any objects of your
Christian Charity if the good example of your compassionate Brethren may be any
measure of yo^ ^ctions; if the common Law of Nature be at all obligatory; yea,
if the Commands, Promises and Threats of GOD himself throughout the Holy
Scriptures be of any force with you, then think and meditate on these things.

It is very remarkable, That when Our Savior would make a lively description
and representation of the great Day of Judgment, he seems to take no other
measure for the final sentence of Condemnation or Absolution, then the pure
performance of omission of actions of Charity toward his poor distressed
Members. As for example, I was hungry, and ye gave me meat, or ye gave me none:
I was thirsty, and ye gave me drink, or ye gave me none: I was naked, and ye
clothed me, or ye clothed me not: sick and in prison, and ye visited me, or ye
visited me not. Matthew. 25.34, 35, 36, 42, 43.

Wherefore I say, if on the one side you would not swallow down riches to vomit
them up again; if you would not have the increase of your houses to depart; nor
in the fullness of your sufficiency be in want; if you would not have your
riches to corrupt, and your garments to be moth-eaten; if you would not have
your gold to canker, and the rust thereof to witness against you at the last
day: And on the other side, if you your selves would willingly find pity and
relief in the day of your calamity; if you would be preserved in six troubles,
and in seven have no evil touch you; if you would have the loins of the poor
and needy to bear witness for you another day; and the blessing of him that was
ready to perish to come upon you; if you would be blessed in your Houses, and
in your Children; in your cattle, and in your fields, in your barns, and in
your store: In a word, if you would assure your selves either of the comforts
of this, or the treasures of a better life, then turn the deaf ear to all the
suggestions of Satan, the world, or your own carnal and covetous Principles:
Then cast your bread upon the waters, and you shall find it after many days:
Then give a portion to seven, and also to eight, not knowing what evil may come
upon the earth: Then give, and it shall be given to you again, in full measure,
heaped up and running over, for every Omer an Ephah; and for every grain, a
talent. Be assured, that not the value of a cup of cold water that you give to
these poor creatures in the name of Disciples, shall ever lose its reward. No,
no, whoever thou art, if thou draw out thy soul to the hungry, and satisfy the
afflicted soul, then shall thy light rise in obscurity, and thy darkness shall
be as the noon day: If thou cover the naked, and hide not thy self from thy own
flesh; Then shall the Lord guide thee continually, and satisfy thy soul in
drought, and make fat thy bones; Then shall thy righteousness go before thee,
and the glory of the Lord shall be thy rereward. I say rest assuredly
confident, that the God and Father of mercies shall recompense this your loving
kindeness, and repay you a thousand fold into your bosoms, either in this life,
or that which is to come.

This is the Faith, and this is the hearty Prayer of Your most humble and
affectionate servant in CHRIST JESUS, Samuel Morland.

FINIS.
